RATING: 7
*Rating based on playing the iOS app.

When I first saw a review of Ascension, I was unimpressed.  I didn't like the art at all, and the gameplay seemed simplistic and shallow.  Eventually I picked up the game for the iOS and found I really enjoyed it.  I've since played hundreds of times.  Ascension is a great app, but I'm less enthralled with it as a tabletop game (in which format I've only played it once).  It's not bad in cardboard form, but it's just so much quicker and easier in digital format, and doesn't lose anything in that transition that other tabletop games do.

The game system is solid and the Realms Unraveled expansion really takes it from being a good game to being a great game, but when it comes to game night, I just don't see this game hitting the table much.  I'll just stick to playing it on my phone when I have a few minutes to kill.

RATING: 3
Multiplayer: The obvious problems are that the game doesn't last long enough for the cards to have much meaning beyond their VP, and that the game fails if all the central cards are predominately of one type or the other, a situation which may well last for a significant number of player-turns. Our first game was a bit of a joke as the great majority of the monsters never entered play. Some expensive cards are likely to be near-useless, except for their high VP.

It appears to work well two-player, but not otherwise.

RATING: 8
Another game that I consistently win against the Other Player. But somehow I think this time I earned it (sorry, mate!).

I love Ascension. Ascension seems like the perfect answer to everything I dislike about Magic: the Gathering. Which is mainly the price and the sheer endlessness of the game. Nothing wrong about the people playing it (cheers, mate!). 

However, whoever created this game was obviously insane. This whole Energy-thing is fun, but from a scientific perspective it is completely mad. Imagine the concept in real life - just because you have enough energy to power your computer, it means you can recycle the energy to also run the fridge and the heater and of course your toy helicopter (don't ask). That's just ... not how physics works. Who comes up with this madnesss? Even in your average fantasy novel people drain their mana/chi/mindpower/whatever sources. Stuff gets used up. Entropy increases. Does nobody proofread (proof...play?) games anymore?

Then again, it's so much fun to fire up you fifth construct and completely destroy everything on your enemies side of the table ... what do I care about thermodynamics?

RATING: 5
The aesthetics were a pleasant surprise; the art is unique, the crystals are a nice touch, and I've got a soft spot for the dark slant of early second millennium Christianity. Clearly this was a slick iOS production, but it was also a game that looked good on the table, with largish print and a consistent vibe. 

Mechanically, there isn't much to do. Essentially, you're trying to manage how much time you have to escalate your rune count against the need to be able to destroy monsters that clog the row. Unfortunately, the six-card centre row is too random to be able to plan accurately. 

The iOS application made this accessible and even addictive, and I wrote a few strategy articles after several hundred plays: [thread=687646][/thread] and [thread=683177][/thread]. Still, once you understand the set, decisions are mostly automatic, with early deck-thinning great (as usual) and most games between experienced players becoming a crapshoot. Fun for a while, but given how expandable the game has become, difficult to find opponents for the first set, which was lean and quick enough to make it worthwhile for a few months of play. 

Overall, I just don't enjoy the amount of variance in deckbuilding games -particularly this or something like [thing=147020][/thing]. The shuffle creates randomness, but it also feels pointless. Also, this is strictly a 2er game. 

I'D RATHER WE PLAYED: [thing=43022][/thing]. A bit of a lateral comparison, but the choices are meaningful and explicable as a quanitifiable risk. In games like Ascension, you learn the set and then you hope your cards come up.

RATING: 8
Physical:
[thing=158976][/thing]
(Chronicle of the Godslayer, Return of the Fallen, Promos)
[thing=215616][/thing]

Digital:
Chronicle of the Godslayer
Return of the Fallen
Storm of Souls
Immortal Heroes
Rise of Vigil
Darkness Unleashed
Realms Unraveled
Dawn of Champions
Dreamscape
War of Shadows
Promo Packs 1-5

:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
A deck-building game with a central market row. Start with five cards, play some, get some, discard. Repeat. Yes, this form of deck-building has effectively become a board game trope, but Ascension grabbed it by the horns and just turned the dial to Spinal Tap levels of 11. Played with more than three players (but really, played with more than [i]two [/i]players) it becomes swingy and overly luck-based (so yes, I'll agree with you there, Dominion purists), but with two players it is a very tight, high-agency battle for supremacy, both tactical and strategic.

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
So what does Ascension offer over other deck-building games? [b]WOMBO COMBO.[/b] This game more than any like it allows for the setup of absolutely absurd combo plays, with cards triggering cards triggering cards triggering cards for huge gains in economy, card draw, and scoring. My favorite aspect of this is the "multi-unite" feature of many cards, in which a card of one type has a bonus effect by itself, then you get to repeat the bonus effect if another card of a like type is played (even retroactively!). It is not unlike the gif below, if the first card played (which starts a multi-unite domino effect) is Samus, your opponent is the gray Starfox, and the other cards of your deck (which continue to trigger the multi-unite domino effect and add new multi-unite effects of their own) are the green Starfox:

[img]https://media.giphy.com/media/mRbXaJKWlb1SM/giphy.gif[/img]
In two player games, "honor" (aka POINTS) are capped at sixty, but this total can be exceeded if a single turn warrants pushing that envelope (depleting the stated honor pool triggers the end of the game at the close of that round). Here is a great example:

[imageid=3280877 medium] 
[imageid=3493274 medium]
What's that clot of barely-distinguishable rectangles in the bottom half of the top image? Those are all of the cards I played in that SINGLE turn. Remember how I said you start each turn by drawing only five cards? Right. Wombo Combo, indeed.

I like every other deck-building game less for each session of Ascension I play.

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
Certain card interactions can be diluted if you blend lots of expansions or card sets together. This can also increase the swingyness of some games as one player may acquire the only few cards that combo together, while the other player is left with strong but uninteractive one-offs. 

As an added note, yes, the majority of my plays are digital, but I do play the physical game quite often. The accounting of aforementioned combo breaking gets a bit difficult in the analog version, so there's that.

[hr]
11/16/17 EDIT: I moved the rating down from 10 to 8 primarily because I basically only play this via app. While it is my favorite deck-builder, no question, it's unfair (in my opinion) to rank this "10" when I don't really play it as a board game any longer.

RATING: 7
An interesting Dominion variant that has been negatively reviewed by many for what it is not.

Hardcore gamers who want a deep, totally balanced game might want to steer clear of this one.

That said, my partner and I love this game for what it is.

It is:
- a really quick deck building game that allows you to scratch the itch in a short amount of time
- a really simple game to teach to casual and non gamers
- a game with a moderate amount of randomness / luck, which allows less experienced gamers a chance to do well and even win against a more experienced player.

Some issues
- the art: some people hate it. I don't love it, but it has grown on me after awhile. At the least I think most people will find it tolerable.
- card variety: there is indeed a limited variety here. With several plays you would have seen them all. The randomness of the draw helps to make this better, but I definitely see the need for new cards to liven things up.

Do your research to see if it fits your bill, and if you head in with the right expectations you may well end up liking it too.

RATING: 5
A much more tactical game than Dominion or Thunderstone, with which it otherwise shares a lot of the mechanics. In fact, I feel it may be too much so: you try to get the most with the hand you have and what's available, but there's usually only two or three options to pick from. For a supposedly deck building game, there's so few choices and strategy, you're really just adding cards to your deck rather than building anything: on your turn, you look at your cards, if you have 5 points then you buy card A, otherwise you buy card B. 

Only played once, but for what it's worth the impression I was left with is this is much more luck driven than other similar games; in the end, the game's playing you more than the other way around. All that said, it plays very fast and it's still fun, if somewhat shallow.

RATING: 7.5
I only played the iOS version of the game, and it is one of the best conversions of a boardgame to an app I ever experienced. Playing deckbuilding games without the annoyance of the reshuffling is indeed better!
Ascension is one of the first Dominion clones to appear on the market, and used a particular twist on the variable set up of the game: the card display is ever-changing!
I like it better than Dominion, but the complete randomness in setting the available cards up is a possible downside: strategy cannot be conceived and one must adapt to what fate offers.
I suspect the iOS version would deserve a 7.5 (the randomness is not a problem in a 5-10 minutes game), but the actual game is a 7 (with the shuffling it cannot last less than 30 min.)...

Update november 9, 2012: the real cardboard game is not so fiddly, so it deserves a 7.5.

RATING: 8.2
Fantasy deckbuilding game that's just that bit different because of some cleverly designed aspects. The constant changing pool of cards you are able to purchase is a major attraction of the game. Managing this pool is very important. You should get the cards you need and banish cards from the pool that could potentially hurt you. The constructs are also a nice addition to the system and really make it stand apart. The gameplay is quick and involved. While Dominion is almost entirely strategical, this game is very tactical. You still have to manage your deck quality and quantity though. Good card quality and great illustrations make for a well rounded solid game.

Gameplay: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::halfstar:
Fun/Social: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Components: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Rules: :star::star::star::star::nostar:

RATING: 4
Slow to get started, quick to end.  It is a tactical game.  It has hard to have any consistent strategy.  It usually feels like your cards are stalling.

I read 2 variants that might be helpful:
* 3 rows of 4 where cards in row 2 cost +1, and row 3 cost +2.  Replace from the row above and fill row 3 from the deck.
* Use two rounds of spending 4 rune to buy anything from the deck.  After select 10 cards from the starter deck and the bought cards as your true starter deck.

I played some 'solo' by using 2d6 to simulate another player.  After each of my turns I rolled and placedthe cards that appeared in the numbered slots to the 'opponent' pile.  I used 30 honor as a pool. When all the honor the pool was acquired, I total my honor and compared it to the opponent.  It works out ok.  May need some tweaks to balance it out so that the 'opponent' wins more.

RATING: 6
This is like the bastard lovechild of Dominion and Magic the Gathering. The four 'factions' feel a lot like Magic colours - each has a theme that you can rely on in deckbuilding. Even though there's a LOT of luck (and there is a LOT), purchasing the right faction's cards at the right time can really change your game. More depth than it pretends to have, less than you'd want for prolonged play. That being said, me and my girlfriend play it 3 or 4 times in a row most nights we play games. A great filler game for Dominion fans.

EDIT: After 14 plays, mainly 2 player, I've realised that the game is so  much better 2 -player that it's not really worth playing 3 or 4 player. By the time you add the fourth player, all forward planning disappears and (as one of my playgroup said) "the game plays you". To retain any semblance of strategy or planing, keep it to two players.

I've also come to the conclusion that 200 cards just isn't enough. You can memorize the abilities and costs of a 200-card set pretty quickly, and the game becomes quickly repetitive once you have.

EDIT AGAIN: Played a good 4-player tonight - this game punishes noobs like very few games out there, but the more experience you have with it the more you'll get out of it. I retract the statement above about 4-player being rubbish, also I've added a half-point to its rating.

I'd listed it for trade, thinking I'd played it out, but tonight I realised it's still fun - I think I'll take it off the trade list.

EDIT3: Hasn't hit the table in a long time. Not really all that interested anymore - up for trade again.

EDIT4: Traded away jul '11 for extra deck of martell cards for aGoT LCG.

RATING: 5
Calling this game designed is somewhat of as stretch.  This is yet another deck-building game with the gimmick that the cards up for purchase are random.  So the mechanics are well worn with a bad idea thrown in to make it new and different.  The random card purchases along with the rather large deck sizes completely obscures any attempts at a coherent strategy.  

Which is fine because the cards themselves were developed without any synergy at all.  The Mechana cards are a half-hearted attempt, but I would really like to see someone succeed at a Mechana strategy.  Instead the "designers" just came up with a huge pile of generic cards and threw them in a deck.  As such the best play on any round is to purchase the best card without reference to whatever may be in your deck.

Why waste your time with this when you can play any number of other games in this burgeoning genre?

RATING: 5
Just another lack-luster deck building game.  Players buy or capture/kill cards from a 5-card tableau in order to collect VP's or better cards for their personal decks.  When one card is taken (bought or banished), it is immediately replaced with a random card from the tableau draw deck.  The game is full of boom and bust... either you have what you need to acquire cards or you don't (and cannot do much on your turn).  It's an okay way to pass the time periodically, but I don't need to own it and probably won't suggest to play it.  The expansions (some of which stand alone) add a bit of variety so I'd prefer to play with them.

** 2014 update: The more I play it, the less I like the base set.  When the standard number of VPs are used, the game ends too quickly... usually just as you dilute the start cards enough that the deck-building become interesting because you begin to see the fruits of your labor.  At least part of the problem is that it's difficult to get rid of the crappy start cards since only 2-3 purchasable cards allow you to repeatedly banish cards from your hand or discard pile.  To me, almost all of the cards in the Ascension base set feel generic and boring... and the gameplay feels sluggish, only ramping up near the end of the game.  In contrast, Ascension: Storm of Souls felt refreshing as if there were more choices/options on your turn and more interesting card powers even though the framework/basic gameplay is the same.

RATING: 7
The latest game to cannibalize the Dominion deck building mechanic.  Ascension plays VERY similarly to Thunderstone, but with more polish overall.  The art design is better, the components are better, and the game flows a little better.

Ascension does a good job of fixing some of the flaws inherent with Dominion.  The cards are designed in a way that makes it difficult to get a truly bad hand, there is always something useful you can do.  The communal purchasing pool adds interactivity and allows for variety, while also requiring less setup/storage time than Dominion.  Plus, (once you get rid of the completely unnecessary board) it's MUCH more portable than Dominion; easily fitting in a pair of Magic deck boxes.

Overall, it's probably the best utilization of the deck building mechanic I've yet seen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Ascension is a pretty fun deckbuilder with lots of variety and core mechanics that are simple enough that the game is very easy to teach to new players. However, the game is not without its problems.
There is a very high random element to this game, especially when compared to other deckbuilders like Dominion or Thunderstone. You may often find yourself needing one key component to your strategy that never comes up in the game, or it will make one appearance in the game but then disappear so suddenly that you never even had a chance to pick it up.
In addition, my play group finds that there is a clear dominating strategy in this version of the game, and the only way to prevent a player utilizing the cards involved from totally walking away with the game is to deliberately tailor your entire approach to hosing that one player, meaning that IF you win the game it is not because you played well or had a good strategy, but that you simply adapted to the other guy. This tends to be an unsatisfactory way to win the game, in my opinion.
Overall, a positive gaming experience due to the variety of the cards, but be aware that the random element is very strong.

RATING: 5
Compares unfavorably to Dominion. It lacks the 'story arc' imparted by the switch to VP's that makes Dominion interesting - all the cards in Ascension are good, and give VP commensurate with their cost & ability. You never have to fear getting too many VP cards too early and crippling your deck. This causes the game to lack strategic decisions, which is compounded by the fact that to the randomness of the card draw is added the randomness of the 6 face-up cards. You don't get the benefit of concentrating on a smaller pool from among the possible choices - having every card (possibly) available every game makes it much harder to set up combos. Overall, I'm not really a fan, but it's OK to play on iPad occasionally.

Also, Mechana is way too strong. The fact that the computer AI (and I) preferentially acquire any Mechana cards we can, especially constructs, no matter what else is going on in that particular game means they're overpowered. The subsequent sets don't address this; some make it worse.

I play this on the iPad quite a bit but I'd never buy it and would resist playing in real life.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's an okay game, but the dual currency (runes to purchase cards and power to fight monsters) together with random cards in the center row create a lot of frustration. Having a hand full of one kind of currency and seeing only the other kind of cards appear in the center row happens all too often. Buying both currencies means that you will often have mediocre hands with a little of both.

There is also no limit on how many actions you can play (like in Dominion) so there are almost no cards that you do not want to have in your deck. If a card draws a card or is better than the standard non-center-row cards it's good to have it in your deck. Also all cards give victory points. That reduces the amount of difficult decisions in the game, and a lot of turns are obvious (buy the only or best you can afford, or defeat the only or strongest monster). The main decision in many games seems to be whether to buy Heavy Infantry or not. 

It's not very deep and almost multiplayer solitaire, but somehow it's still an enjoyable activity if played with few players (we made the terrible mistake sometimes of playing with more than 4 players with the expansion - that really ruins the game IMO.)

I really like the art though.

RATING: 4
Take Thunderstone and make it even MORE random.  If you hate it when you get a powerful hand in Thunderstone and there are only wimpy monsters to kill in the dungeon, you will really hate this game because there are many turns when there are NO monsters to kill (other than the Cultist "booby prize").  Knowing that going in, you skew your deck to be mostly rune (money) generating instead of combat power, but it sucks when it happens - and it happens frequently.

I can't help but compare this game to Thunderstone, because they are so similar, and Ascension really pales in comparison.  There are much fewer cards, so there is less variety in gameplay.  There are few card combos, and even the ones in the game are "iffy" to bother trying for because the other card(s) you need could be at the bottom of the draw pile and will never come out.  So the game boils down to buying the best cards that you can each turn and hoping for the best.  I'm not saying Thunderstone has a lot of strategy, but at least it has SOME strategy (go for light or not, go for card combos, etc.).  Ascension has ZERO strategy.

There's also a runaway leader problem.  Thunderstone shares this problem, but it isn't as pronounced.  There are a few powerful cards in Ascension that when purchased early on really supercharge your deck - like the "draw 3 cards" hero or some of the void cards that lets you draw cards and also thin your deck.  The player lucky enough to have those cards available during their turn will buy them and their deck will then start generating better turns, which enables buying more and better cards, and this cycle continues until the game ends and they've far outpaced their opponents.  In Thunderstone players are limited to claiming one card per turn (generally) so a "super turn" doesn't create nearly the leap ahead that it does in Ascension.

Frankly, I see no reason to play Ascension.  Play Thunderstone instead.  Ascension is quicker, but it is so random that the gameplay feels superfluous and it's really disappointing to me to play a game that is often decided buy a lucky draw or two.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A very cool twist on the deck-building genre, introducing a bit more tactical play if you're into that kind of thing.  Each turn, your available pool of cards is going to change, so even though you can go in with a strategy, you must adapt to both the other players as well as the card selection on your turn.

I also enjoyed the "constructs" which stay face-up in front of you when you buy one (unless someone else is able to get rid of it).  They really help you pull off some bigger turns, reducing total dependence on good draws.

Overall, this is a deck-builder I would always be happy to play!  It will draw the obvious comparisons to Dominion, but it's a different beast which does a nice take on the deck-building mechanic.

RATING: 4
Updated 12/14/10. 
1 play.
4 players.  

Ascension is an intriguing attempt to refine the now-ubiquitous Dominion deck-building formula by adding a more cohesive fantasy narrative.  The game is ultimately doomed, however, by a disappointing lack of control.  

Ascension's first noticeable departure from Dominion comes in the game's lack of restrictions: you may play and purchase as many cards as your hand allows each turn.  The cards in Ascension also adds to Dominion's basic formula by provide players with [i]two[/i] currencies to manage. (I know, right?)  The first, power, is essentially a player's military might on any given turn.  The second, runes, are the game's money.  Both runes and power are "expended" each turn through the purchase of new cards or the slaying of monsters, respectively. 

This brings me to Ascension's greatest design drawback: the cards available for purchase and monsters available for slaying comprise a randomly drawn pool of six cards (from a deck of 100+, it appears).  So you're using a randomly drawn hand to make the best of a small, randomly drawn set of options.  If all you have is runes, and the pool is nothing but monsters, sucks for you.  If all you have is power, and the pool is nothing but artifacts, sucks for you.  Although the game provides weak escape valves in these instances, the impact of these moments on the players' enjoyment of the game is likely much greater than the impact of these moments on the final scores.

Relatedly, and as damningly, the random pool makes deck-building aspect of this deck-building game feel rather trivial.  Because of the limited card selection available to players on each turn, it is impossible to have any meaningful future intentions at the time you make this turn's purchase.  Sure, you can try to pick the card that works best with what you've already accumulated, but of course you also need to be able to afford it with the hand you've drawn.  This is a particularly unfulfilling form of deck-building, particularly when the game's apparent innovation was narrative consistency.  

As a extremely novice strategy point, it seems that a runes-heavy approach is all but futile.  In contrast to purchasing, slaying monsters doesn't add cards to your deck while producing victory points.  A lean deck allows for your select few purchases to reappear more often, meaning that you can actually exploit card synergies, occasionally.  It seems that the meaningful strategic decision in this game comes in deciding between a 60/40 power advantage or an 80/20 one. 

With so few truly meaningful decisions, and thematic trappings that don't really speak to me (do I really care whether I have a Void Hero or a Mechancha Hero?), this is certainly not a game that I have any interest in exploring further.  That said, it's familiar enough and innocuous enough that I wouldn't begrudge a friend who insists on bringing it to the table every now and again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I regularly have a couple of friends together and we play games such as this.

I have to admit, I'm really not one for card games and the last one that I played was Pokemon in elementary school so I was really not looking forward to this one.

I gave it a chance and I was really glad I did. I really did enjoy it and despite having 3 other games to choose from, we wound up playing this one the entire night. 

The 2nd round was enjoyable but we wanted to add a little bit of a challenge to the game for the last game so we changed it up a little:

+All bloods (5 victory point tokens) and diamonds (1 victory point tokens) were in play. This made the game somewhat longer
+Instead of 6 monster/constructs/hero cards out in the center at one time, we had 12, but we couldn't replenish once we bought, we had to chose from what was out at the beginning of our turn then replenish the center once our turn was over. This really lengthened the game because we had to read each card before planning on what to do. Often times we would have to re-read them during our turn since we may make a move with part of our hand then re-evaluate with the remainder of our hand.

In short, our modification added another factor to the game. That being the difficulty in keeping up with what was out and also the elimination of cards from the center was purely to impact another player, not to see what would come up in hopes of purchasing a better card.

Whether or not the change is for you should be determined by first playing a round of it normally then deciding if you are in a mood to haggle other player's turns by impacting through elimination of center cards AND if you have enough time.

Regardless of everything, I really ended up enjoying the game though I never won it.

RATING: 2
Revisited this (via iOS) after finding Star Realms pleasantly tolerable.

Still don't get the appeal of this. At all.

My complaints:

1. Ascension is billed as a more thematic alternative to Dominion. It's not, except by the silly definition of "thematic" that equates that term with "stuff Ritalin-deprived teenaged boys like." You could paste the same mechanics on 100 themes.

2. Its advocates point to it being more tactically interesting because of the need to balance two currencies instead of Dominion's one. True enough for what that's worth; except Dominion does not, in point of fact, have one currency. It has money, actions, buys, and additional card draws, in the base game alone. Ascension has buy-cards points, kill-enemies points, and card draws. By eliminating the limitations on taking multiple actions or buying multiple things per turn, Ascension pointlessly hobbles not only the turn-by-turn tactical questions (is there EVER a sound strategic reason to not play every card in your hand?) but also limits the diversity of possible card designs.

3. Ascension is also talked up for removing the Dominion mechanic by which points, when bought, become ballast in your hand, thus making the game faster and easier, to which I say that chess would be made faster and easier if you replaced every pawn with a queen; the burden of proof is on those who claim this makes the game better. 

4. The larger point is that the turn-by-turn card play is the decidedly less interesting aspect of Dominion as a game; a large percentage of the strategic challenge of Dominion is in the moment after the kingdom cards hit the table and before the first cards are played, when you sit and puzzle out an overall strategy. In Ascension, you can have every intention in the world of building your strategy around a key combo but if the cards don't come up at the right time, tough noogies for you.

5. Devil's Adv.: "And yet, this same sort of unpredictability, this dynamic of taking what the draw deck gives you and trying to Make Lemonade -- doesn't that also happen in Race For The Galaxy and Pax Porfiriana, and don't you love those games?" A reasonable point, DA. The difference, as I see it, is that, when the bullet hits the bone, those games afford the player multiple reasonable through-lines to victory. In Ascension, your strategic scope is limited to thinning your deck out as much as possible and/or stringing together enough card-drawers to move through your deck as fast as possible, buying shit and killing shit. How you do it is petty details, and yet those petty details are the game.

6. While there's some marginal use of the various card factions providing synergistic bonuses (the main area in which base Star Realms surpasses base Ascension), in the base game at least this mechanic seems woefully underdeveloped. It's possible this is fleshed out in the expansions, but finding this out would necessitate buying the expansions, a proposition on which your faithful reviewer forcefully demurs.

7. My opinion of the artwork has, uh, not aged like a fine wine. Fifteen minutes of looking at cards that look like concept art for dodgy heavy metal albums has my poor bedraggled eyes crying for something, anything more pleasant. "Less grotesque than Tanto Cuore" is a totally reasonable checklist item for an art director, much like "Doesn't explode without warning" is a good checklist item for a car designer. But man's reach should exceed his etc., and reaching the baseline goals, while well and good, should not preclude taking a whack at something greater. Obviously, your mileage can and will vary on this point.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Ascension is a very solid deck-building game. It shares some characteristics with Dominion, but it is far from a clone. It's a game about reacting to the current cards that are available instead of planning a long-term strategy. It plays quickly, sets up quickly, and the mechanics are easy to learn and understand. Ascension shines as a two-player game. With more players it is still enjoyable but the random element increases significantly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
R's
At first glance, Ascension is a deck building game much like Domninion.  Unlike Dominion, it has two different kinds of currency.  One kind may buy monsters or victory points.  The other may purchase heroes or constructs.  Heroes or constructs may provide either or both kinds of currency, and may have other advantages as well, e.g. banishing cards from your deck.  Buying (called conquering) a monster may grant victory points or other advantages.  Some affect a player's opponents.  

It is also unlike Dominion in that the cards available to by are chosen from a set of 6.  Once one is gone, it is replaced.  Dominion, by contrast, has a library of cards available that does not change during the game.

Like many card games, doing well in Ascension depends more on tactics than strategy.  The best strategic plan comes to naught if the cards necessary for it never become available.

With that said, this game may be too luck-dependent for my taste.  It may happen that cards combine poorly in your hand and thus there is never quite enough of either type.  It may also happen that the cards available are too expensive to acquire, or that powerful cards seem to come to the market on other player's turns.  

The game is about 30 minutes, so I will try it a few more times and grade it with a hopeful rating of 6.  I am not confident this rating will hold.

RATING: 8

RATING: 7.5
For trade in UK Math Trade or BGG Trade.

Components checked.

This fantasy-themed deck building game is quick to learn and is a great game to buy if you're new to deck building games.  

The artwork is really divisive: some love it, some hate it!  Personally I like it.  The artwork gets better with subsequent expansions.

All of the sets in Ascension are not particularly deep or strategic.  There is randomness in the way Ascension plays.  But I love the Ascension sets because they're quick to set-up, the  turns are quick, the ease of play, trying new sets mixed together is fun and being able to play cool combo turns when you get some really good cards in your deck is so much fun.

Storm of Souls has more variety to it than the original Chronicles of the Godslayer (re-named as "Ascension: Deckbuilding Game"), so Storm of Souls might be a better starting choice if you're a more experienced deck building gamer.

RATING: 6
This is a decent deckbuilder, but it falls short of games such as Dominion and Thunderstone due to its higher element of randomness thanks to the much-maligned market row mechanic. In those two games, the bulk of the randomness occurs prior to the start of the game, then you can develop a winning strategy based on the cards on offer. 

In Ascension, with the available card pool constantly changing, developing and sticking to a cohesive strategy can be difficult, and not being offered the cards you need can be a frustrating experience. With Thunderstone and Dominion as options, I don't see this one hitting the table very much.

RATING: 5
One play in. Meh, I'm not impressed. I didn't find it anywhere near as interesting as [i]Thunderstone[/i] or [i]Dominion[/i]. On most turns my choices felt as obvious as they were limited, and victory depended entirely on where and when the cards fell, rather than on any particular choices I made.

RATING: 6.5

It was.... okay.... I suppose.

Not horrible, but nothing new/great.

One thing that I can say is that the rulebook is atrociously written... they leave out basic things that you'd have no idea what to do.

For instance:

You flip out a new card onto the main row immediately after purchasing/destroying/etc one. THIS WAS NOWHERE IN THE RULEBOOK!

Barry and I were like... "okay... wtf? When do you replace the card. After turn, before turn...??" We had no idea until he looked it up on BGG.

Also, as it compares to Dominion:

Dominion has the ABCD turn order.... Ascension doesn't have that fluid order... you can really play all your cards face up in front of you and basically do everything at once in any order... it doesn't freakin' matter... Not that it matters as much in Dominion, but the order in which you play your action cards matter.... Ascension (that I understand) simply doesn't care.

RATING: 7.5
This is a very good deck building game.  It is quite simple, and also has several layers of depth that are revealed with multiple plays.  I was dubious to begin with because I figured nothing would live up to the standard of Dominion, but this game is worth playing.  It is a little more random than Dominion because of how the available cards might not line up with your draw (in other words, there are two shifting variables instead of one, leading to more volatility in the luck of the draw), but it has a lot of skill none the less.  

If Ascension had come out before Dominion (or booster drafting), I probably would have rated it a full point higher.  Instead, I'm already familiar with most of the concepts, which means it was less novel and it took less time for me to figure out how to play well.

--- EDIT ---
I increased the rating from 7.5 to 8.  The game has more skill than I at first gave it credit for.  A lot of time was put into thinking the cards through and balancing them.  The value of each cards changes depending upon the cards in your deck and the length of time left in the game.  I should have expected more from my fellow Magic player game designers.  Kudos!

RATING: 4
Ascension is a deck-building game along the same lines of Dominion or Thunderstone, but rather than a variable set of 10 types of kingdom cards that you can build in any given game, it has a set of cards that are randomly drawn from a deck as old ones are purchased. Three cards are always available for purchase to add consistency. To add additional complexity to the game, there are two types of currency to manage: runes, which let you buy cards, and power, which lets you kill monsters for one time special effects and victory points. The game continues until a pile of victory point chips, which varies based on the number of players, is emptied, with the most points winning.

I was an early adopter of Dominion but, after about 70 plays, decided that it was ultimately too repetitive and not nearly tense enough for my tastes. I did not find the interactions to be interesting and greatly preferred card games like Race For the Galaxy or Glory to Rome. Ascension seemed different enough from Dominion that it looked like it was worth getting into, but the overall lack of ability to build any real strategy killed the game for me. I don‚Äôt have a problem with more tactical games, games that are mostly simple deck-building, and perhaps economic snowball games in general, are not for me.

This game has found a bit of a new life for me on iOS. It is a game I can play while doing other things, and probably epitomizes the pick up, play, and put down ease of use that makes iOS games great. My rating on iOS is probably a 5 or 6. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
A tactically-focused deckbuilder. I played once in person and enjoyed it, but after ~20 more games, the flaws become more apparent. The game simply doesn't afford enough space for cards to be valued in meaningfully different ways.

Initially, you'll see and learn the tradeoff between the acquisition of powerful cards and the acquisition of cards useful only for honor. As time goes on, though, it becomes fairly clear that this tradeoff is clear for the vast majority of the game, only providing meaningful decisions in a slim minority of cases.

The rune/power split also seems to provide interest at first, but again the decisions become very simple as the game goes on. Building decks that can meaningfully provide both resources at the same time is a mug's game, and there's no major benefit for being able to do both at once other than the occasional clogged row. So the game's dual-currency, rather than being a force of strength, really just further constrains your turn-by-turn decision-making options. It's more than a bad design decision; it's one entirely opposed to the game's supposedly tactical focus. 

The game isn't entirely without merit; there's some interesting factional synergies, and the playthrough of a given hand is sometimes enjoyable. That said, the game's flaws outweigh its merits, and it's entirely a forgettable experience.

I'm approaching this game as a tactical game; some will point to the game's supposed thematic strengths. Really, though, the game's theme is practically nonexistent. Acquired-and-spent power, runes used to purchase cards, and the rotating center row are given no justification (they don't need one, as the game is entirely a tactical decision-making exercise, but I think it's worth addressing the claim that the game is thematic).

I actually enjoyed the artwork and design of the original, but it looks like the newest version has removed whatever visual distinction the game had in favor of a blander style with more popular appeal.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really love bizarre mythological and fairy tale style folk art, and that pretty much sums up the art in this game.

I really love trying to figure out the best way to build decks around certain cards.  I really love that there are a few different ways to get points and you need to choose what is going to work best for your deck.  

I found drafting and controlling the center row to be much more interactive than other deck builders.  The game seemed easy and pretty solitary the first few times around because every card does something, there aren't any deck cloggers, and all non-basic cards are worth points. But it eventually clicked, and I realized it's as much about building decks as it is manipulating the center row (which is the essential function of your deck).  

Players don't have equal opportunity, and that's what makes this game so interesting - you need to make openings for yourself while trying to set up a center row that fustigates your opponent.  

I love it!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Yes yes, it is another Dominion clone that does some things better and some worse.

Pros: Much smoother gameplay (less "mathy" counting of buys and actions). More variety while playing the game (a whole deck of different cards instead of 10 stacks). Easier after game clean up.

Cons: Clunkier scoring (I dislike having EACH card worth victory points AND having a VP pile), Random purchases at any given time (the more players the less you can plan a deck build). Definite advantage to going first with more players (all cheap cards can be bought before the last player, leaving him with the disadvantage of having expensive cards and monsters he can not buy/attack on turn one).

In all, I wouldn't buy it, but would play someone else's copy on occasion.

RATING: 8
Very fun Game, very similar to Dominion. Like Dominion, there are multiple strategies one could employ to ultimately win the game. However, the base game is less than half the number of cards as Dominion, so the number of possible strategies, and overall replay value is lowered. There were three things I personally found more enjoyable in Ascension than Dominion: Ascension has more player interaction, and one player's turn has a much bigger impact on the next player's turn; Ascension's theme is more entertaining, the background is much richer than Dominion, and you can tell that the designers took influence from Magic: The Gathering; finally Ascension is substantially faster to set up and play, which is always a plus in my book. This is a really good game for collectible card game player that bridges the gap between a game like Magic: The Gathering and a board game.

RATING: 9
(Rating including expansion: [thing=157026]Realms Unraveled[/thing]; Rating also based on hundreds of plays on the app)

This is a great deckbuilder. Mainly for three reasons:

1) You can make some insane combos! I've got the app to crash on several occassions because I played 30+ cards in a single turn.

2) Setup is really fast! This is a big plus for me.

3) All the cards you acquire are worth points and there are also crystals you can acquire directly or by beating up monsters that are worth points. So you don't have victory point cards clogging up your deck.

The one downside: It is ugly. Not for lack of a better term. Really ugly. Also, there are much better sets than the original one, so this rating is explicitly for Ascension including expansions. The base game by itself would probably be a 7.

RATING: 6
I think out of the 3 similar games, at this time the other two being Dominion & Thunderstone, I will place this one in the middle.  This is the easiest to play and takes far less time to teach newbies, which is in its favor.  At this time I am rating it lower than Dominion because I feel the luck element is much stronger and some cards are overpowering, especially in combination with others. Although it does have far more luck than Thunderstone, because the play is far simpler and setup is faster as well as play, I rate it above Thunderstone.

I enjoy all 3, and glad I own all three. 

Have ordered box & sleeves from Board & bits

Of course expansions will be coming, and hopefully additional cards will balance out the game better.

Edit: I have raised the rating since this one has continued to hit the table more often than many other games.  I have enjoyed it more with repeated play.

EDIT:  I recently placed an order from the publisher's web site, and had a complaint which I registered by email.  Gary responded immediately with apologies and corrected the problem with a very surprising bonus.  This is a top notch company who cares about their product and customers, and they go the extra mile. Thank you, so much!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Ascension is another deck-building game trying to stand on the shoulder of Dominion. Supposedly, deck-building is about long-term strategy. After all, if you could simply restructure your entire deck from turn to turn that entire aspect would be kind of pointless.

Ascension looks promising at first because there are several factions which often provide nice benefits when used with other cards of the same faction so some sort of specialization looks to be the way to go. Makes sense so far? What doesn't make sense, however, is that the game then kills every attempt at exploiting those combos by providing a randomly drawn pool of cards to purchase from or fight for points each turn. Whether the cards you need for your strategy will ever come up is unknown. There goes your strategy.

Instead, the game degenerates into micro-optimizing each individual turn which offers neither interesting decisions nor any sort of control.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Pros: each game is unique since the center row to choose from is random and there's a large variety of cards. You could say this gives it replayability.

Cons: the game is highly dependent on luck. In some cases (I'd say a good third or more), you can tell who is going to win the game based on the first 2-4 turns. If there's a deck management card that you are able to get to first, you will have a tremendous advantage unless one happens to come up for your opponent(s) early. Likewise with certain constructs. This really detracts from enjoyability.

RATING: 7
Fun game that is amazingly easy to explain and I like the unique artwork. The game is very streamlined, but that doesn't mean the game is too light. I appreciate the design and think it is quite clever. What appeals to me is that you really have to adapt in this game. You get ideas of strategies the more you play, but you still have to be on your toes.

It's a good sign when players wish to play this one again immediately after playing it the first time.

People are whining like crazy about card-size differences, but honestly, you'll want to sleeve your cards anyway and that fixes the problem. Even if you are not into sleeves, it isn't like it matters that much when you are drawing cards from the deck anyway. I understand ocd, but some people are O to the C to the D when it comes to this game's components.

UPDATE: I continue to play it, I continue to enjoy it. They've done a great job with the first two expansions, so I am upping my rating. Each expansion expands on the story and the added mechanics really integrate nicely. Also, the artwork has improved I think with each release.

------------------------------------------------------

I've played this to death on the iPod, and I think it actually plays better there than on the table. There are a lot of different card effects to keep track of every turn, so much so that even after half-a-dozen games with my group, they are forgetting how things work. On the iPod, it keeps track of all your stuff for you. It's one of my most-played games on the iPod because it's light and generally mindless but with a little bit of fun strategy.

The actual game has not gone over as well with my group as I thought it would. I'm not sure why that is; maybe just not enough control or decisions to be made?

RATING: 5
While I didn't actively dislike Ascension, I don't see any reason to play it, because it's inferior to other games in its genre. The constantly churning card menu means there's no capacity for players to plan ahead or think strategically. The decisions all feel very easy, with each turn feeling the same as the previous turn. If I needed to take a bathroom break and asked any random halfway decent gamer to take over my position for a few turns, I wouldn't worry at all. They would probably do exactly the same moves I would have done - the game is that obvious.

[b]UPDATE[/b] Raised rating from 4 to 5. Adding the Storm of Souls expansion and playing strictly with two players does make for a better game. For me, this is an iPad-only game. It's actually [i]outstanding[/i] in that format, where the annoying bookkeeping, shallowness, extreme randomness, and mostly solitaire gameplay are not significant issues.

RATING: 7.5

Firstly, one card was nicked straight from the wrap.
Secondly,two cards ripped during shuffling.

How many other games have thin cards that don't rip like these?  I outta know owning as many games as I do.  Board is slightly warped as well.

I've never bought a game with cards this shxxxy.  I bought high quality sleeves for the game, not from their shameless sleeve gouge, and now it's fine.

My group plays it a lot in substitute of Dominion so raising rating (and I haven't recorded my dozens of plays).  Not as good but quicker. +1

We play it a lot so bumping rating.

RATING: 6
Rubric:
*Components (1-5) - 4 (Standard card stock, the black borders will eventually show signs of wear with play and be easily marked.)
*Artwork (1-5) - 5 (Unique and thematic artwork.)
*Intuitiveness & Replay-ability (1-10) - 9 (If you've played any deckbuilder, you barely need to read the rules.)
*Strategy & Choices (1-10) - 8 (There are limited strategies, all easily attainable.  After a few playthroughs it's obvious some strategies are more effective than others.)
*Game Experience (1-10) - 5 (A little too simplistic for me.  Players who are new to deckbuilders might find this a good gateway game.  It plays the same solitaire as it does competitive.  There is little social aspect to the game nor is there much of a way to effect the other player(s) on or outside of your turn.)
*Elegance (1-10) - 10 (This game may suffer from too much playtesting.  It feels like a reskinning of other deckbuilders with no unique element to give it's own experience.)

RATING: 8
I picked this up on the advice of a friend.  I like this game as a filler between heavier/longer games.  Think of it as a cross between Magic and Dominion.  There is really no direct confrontation as you are building a deck to collect "Honor" to win.  Honor is collected by killing monsters or buying powerful heroes and constructs.  I hated the artwork at first, but then it grew on me the more I played the game.  Bottom line, this is easy to teach, easy to play, and is a quick setup and breakdown.

RATING: 7
I was happily surprise by this one.  Yes, it's another "deck building" type game, but this one is bare bones, and works remarkably well.

Only played one game, which ended in a Triple tie in first place (51 points), with the last player no more than 5 points behind.

The tie breaker is the reverse order of the turn order, but we decided to call it a tie nonetheless.

I did not like the graphics/images, but that just personal taste.  The game play itself is fairly straight forward, especially if you have played other "deck building" type games before.

I liked the row of 6 cards where you can purchase as many cards as you have money, and kill as many monsters as you have attack points.  And there were plenty of "banish" cards which allowed you to thin out your deck.

RATING: 5
1-2-11
In retrospect from my one play of this game, there are a number of things I don't like about this Dominion-esque game.  It's a difficult game to understand right away because of all the randomness and hidden information.  I think that's a great thing about Dominion - all of the possible cards are in the open from the start so there are no surprises and very little randomness.

Once you get used to the card functions, you realize how useless a couple of the mechanisms are.  Constructs end up getting destroyed by other cards by the time you even get one played in front of you.  The colors as far as different 'races' go seemed like a totally useless 'feature' and only served to be more confusing.  Again, comparing to Dominion, color-coding between rune cards and military cards would have been a lot easier and remove some useless fluff.

I thought the art style was pretty mediocre.  Mediocre and difficult to see from across the table since many cards look too similar.  Again beat by its superior, Dominion, in this regard.

The game itself just isn't that fun.  Since the board is entirely random, there is no planning.  Any plans you have for your turn will likely be shot completely by the time you can play again in a 4-player game.  That and the randomness of your deck, supplied by random card picks which are basically just picking the best opportunity.  There is no strategy at all - you simply react to what is out there.  You can lean a certain way perhaps, but leaning is nothing compared to the crunching you can try to do in Dominion.  I keep referring to that game - and it is far superior.  These guys should stick to MtG.

RATING: 8
[i]Comments for the first edition "Godslayer" version, played exclusively 2p against the iPad AI:[/i]

Originally, I hated my surface pass on this one:  the art was ugly; the gameplay simplistic, derivative and limited.

Then, after like a billion plays on the iPad, I finally "got" the art, appreciating the iconic, atavistic religiosity of it*, as well as the game-wide unifying aspect of having a single artist's vision throughout; and the game that seemed so simple on the surface cracked open and exposed vistas of interaction, tactics and strategy.  Every time I think I've topped out with the killer approach I find new pathways that go deeper into weird labyrinths.

To top it off, the fantasy world presented in the game is like peeking into someone's private wank-bank of bizarre fetishes; it is wonderfully nonstandard and "wiggy".

The whole shebang is a triumph of undiluted vision.

UPDATE:  8 --> 7  Honestly, just wore this one out.  It was good for more digital plays than I can count (which really does put it into the "outstanding" category), a fantastic toy while it lasted but I find I'm done with it now.

UP-UPDATE:  7 --> 8  That wiggy world has pulled me back in and I find myself wanting to play constantly‚Äî[COLOR=#FF0000]it's a big badass comic book[/color] filled with interesting personalities and reality-shattering shenanigans.  God help me but I love it...  

The thing itself is a neat distillation of [thing=463]Magic[/thing]'s higher points (though without the attendant depth) of art/world/story, deck building, and the chaining of ridiculous combos.  As a deckbuilder it's the quick-fix version of touching those nubbins in all the ways [thing=36218]Dominion[/thing] doesn't.

As a screen-time noodler it's like someone used my skull as a Play-Doh¬Æ Fuzzy Pumper Barber Shop‚Ñ¢ to squeeze a wad of Solitaire and this is what came curling out of my head holes.

Physical sets owned:

[thing=158976]Year One Collector's Edition[/thing]
(Base game; Fate mechanic)

[thing=108784]Storm of Souls[/thing]/[thing=122294]Immortal Heroes[/thing]
(Events, Trophy Monsters; Soul Gems)

[thing=138233]Rise of Vigil[/thing]/[thing=144864]Darkness Unleashed[/thing]
(Treasure mechanic w/ Energy Shards, Energize mechanic; Dark Energy Shards, Transform mechanic)

[size=7]*Word is the card art is supposed to be snippets from the Grand Tapestry commemorating the events in the game.  Like bits of Bayeux.  [i]Very cool.[/i][/size]

RATING: 7
I passed on this at first, 'cos I felt like I got burned on Thunderstone, and was wary of another deck-building monster-fighter. I didn't like the way that TS said that you were engaging in combat, when really you were just buying monsters using a cludgy formula.

In Ascension, you're also buying monsters (with attack rating "currency"), but it doesn't bother me nearly as much. A lot of that has to do with the lack of cludge, but it might also be 'cos you're simply "defeating" monsters; the lack of combat pretense sits better with me. Then, the results of monster-fighting are just more interesting; their secondary powers make for some good tactical decision-making.

All in all, it's fast, it's fun, and I'm glad I gave it a chance.

***************

A couple of years later, my hundreds and hundreds of plays of this via iOS have killed any desire to play this face-to-face.

RATING: 10
A great game, for a TCG player like myself. A lot of people are comparing this game to Dominion. The game follow the same rules basically but they are completely different games. It is like comparing apples to oranges, some say that there is less strategy in this game that there is in Dominion. Dominion requires premeditation prior to the start of the game you should decided what combination you will want to play, and that part does not really change as the game goes along because noone can really stop you. In Ascension it is the opposite, you can really only play around what you get.

It becomes a matter of taste, I like Ascension more because you have to change and adapt the situation at hand, you can stop other players from buy/defeating the larger cards on the board as well, it feel more interactive.

Another note to have is that Dominion has several expansions ahead of Ascension, if you take the base set of Dominion and play with it, its really not the best... A strong strategy is to just buy gold and buy the larger and better items and just win, it felt like a solitaire game really, the future expansions then fixed that with cards like Pirate Ship (which i hate). Ascension will have expansions to follow and Im sure they will address their issues that people have with the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A bit more random than I'd like.  When I first played this, I thought that the lack of limits on how many cards you play and/or buy on your turn was a good thing.  After playing several more times, though, I've reached the conclusion that all it does is remove the need to make choices - you almost certainly play everything in your hand (things that might banish cards from your hand are an exception), and all that matters is what order you play them in, and sometimes even that doesn't matter.  In Dominion, you had to carefully manage how many actions you bought for your deck, and find a way to play more actions per turn if you were going to need them.  You also had to make a choice about when to buy your victory points.  Ascension doesn't require you to pay any attention to that sort of thing - you just buy the best things you can each turn because you'll always be able to play them all.  Cards you're buying only for their victory point values are almost always relics that you can try to keep out of your deck.  It just feels like a game with fewer meaningful choices than Dominion.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
It's supposedly a card drafting game, but your limited choices are quite random, your hand is random, and some cards are just far better value than others.  There is little synergy between cards, and what there is rarely matters because its too specific.  Can you collect card A and card B, and then get them in your hand before the game is over?  Not likely, especially since your deck just keeps growing in size.  You pretty much must buy cards whenever possible because they are all worth victory points.  A "lean and mean" deck, constructed with thought and careful focus, just doesn't happen.  Not much fun with 2 players and a whole lot worse with 4.

Aug10:

Need some more plays to really cement my feelings.  But the demo was enough to get me to buy it.

It's not *better* than Dominion, but it's plenty good enough and different.

I really like the reduced setup time.  And I like how they did very well with the iconography and the streamlined rules (And lack of exceptions)

You can really combo out on your turn, and I don't think I'd want to play with 4-players, but with 2 or 3 I think you could have some control.

The factions seemed balanced, and there was enough playtesting done I have some faith of this.

You can't precisely plan ahead since the available pool fluctuates.  But like RftG, there are "types" of cards and you can be fairly certain that those "types" will appear at some point.

RATING: 7
A deck-building game.  Since Dominion is one of my favorite games, I naturally evaluate this game through the lens of Dominion.  Ascension seems much more luck-based than Dominion, since you have a limited selection of cards to purchase or defeat, and the selection changes constantly.  It also has a two-tier resource system: some cards give you runes to purchase heroes and artifacts and some give you fight ability to defeat monsters.  Cards are worth points (except your starting cards), and you can also take actions to collect the equivalent of Dominion's VP tokens.  The supply of those is limited in a way very similar to the way the victory tokens in Race for the Galaxy is limited, including it being an end-game trigger.  All in all, it's kind of fun and I wouldn't mind playing it again, but mostly I'll play Dominion instead.

2013-02-28

Initial Rating: 6.0 (February 2013)

Good light game. I know I‚Äôm in the minority here, but I LOVE the art. The theme is also nice although I don‚Äôt feel it when I‚Äôm playing. Ascension is a very easy game to learn and teach, you need to buy cards and/or kill cards, no more. Also it plays very quick.

Unfortunately, the game is not very deep, which can be an advantage if you are playing with your non-gaming friends or want to play something light. I will look into the expansions soon, but for now Dominion continues to be king for me. Also this game has a critical flaw (for me), the central tableau is changing and every turn each player has different opportunities to buy or attack something. I dislike this because it is NOT FAIR and adds a ton of luck. That fact alone leaves the rating at 6.0. Other deck building games ‚Äúguilty‚Äù of this flaw are: Resident Evil, Thunderstone and Legendary. I prefer games like Dominion, in which every player has the same shot at buying a particular card. 

Ascension is best with 2 players, but I will play any number of players.

Bottom line, I wasn‚Äôt expecting a good game, and I was surprised. For the time being I enjoy playing Ascension, but I feel that I will know all that there is to know pretty soon, so I will have to add some expansion to the mix. Also (for now) I prefer to play Ascension over Thunderstone.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 5
Ascension is to Dominion/Thunderstone what Magic Limited is to Constructed.

It isn't that simple a comparison for me though.  I loved Limited formats in Magic because the card base for each set was huge, and there was a wealth of great decisions to be made all the way down the draft.  If I'm going to criticize the CCG format for being too expensive to play regularly, I have to give it credit for providing the massive variety that doesn't exist in an out-of-the-box game like Ascension.

It's harsh to compare Ascension to one of the best games ever though.  This is a decent game in its own right, and holds its own among the growing set of deckbuilding games.  I'd like to play it another few times, but I can already see myself growing bored with it.  Probably not for purchase.

Edit:  I'm a little more skeptical of Ascension after a second play.  Many of the "decisions" seem obvious and it's troublesome that there's so much randomness in who gets early Void cards, for example.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Ascension's contribution to deckbuilding is the random central display of available cards, later to be implemented successfully in Star Realms or Legendary. With this, it was the first quick tactical deckbuilder. Nonetheless, its main success only came with the iOS version. It's among my favorite deckbuilders, avoiding Dominion's strategic trap of simply executing an initial strategy consisting in annoying deck-thinning or action-chaining as well as Thunderstone's potentially too long games.

Compared with its successor in spirit, Star Realms, the factions aren't that differentiated yet, the combo building is not as obvious and massive. The fantasy setting is a bit offbeat and charmingly independent although I'm in the minority with this view as it's mostly considered to be hideous.

RATING: 9
I know I'm probably alone on this, but the fantasy theme does nothing for me.  I'm not sure there's any way to evaluate this game without comparing it to Dominion, and Dominion prevails in every way except for setup time.  

In comparison to Thunderstone, I like that this is simpler, if I had to compare the two I probably prefer this one.

I don't like the changing card layout, which adds a pretty heavy luck element in to the game.  But the main problem I have with the game is the lack of story arc.  I don't know if it's because we were playing wrong, but there was no momentum as we approached the endgame.  It didn't get any faster, the tension did not increase, we just kind of kept doing the same thing over and over again until the endgame conditions were reached.  I'd be willing to try this again to see if it improves, but I'm pretty disappointed.

Update - December 2011
I could have done this a couple months back, but the iPad version of this game completely reversed my opinion.  Getting to know the cards and system better sold me on the game.  Plus the fact that you can play a game in 5 minutes convinced me that this really is a lot of fun.  Because of my change of heart I've had to re-evaluate my approach to deckbuilders in general.  I can't wait for the Thunderstone iOS app, because that was another that I probably didn't give a fair shake.

Up to a 9.

RATING: 7
A game by MtG players for MtG players, borrowing heavily from Dominion. Not so strategic, but a rather tactical game in comparison with Dominion, because of the card pool that functions in a very different way. This is also more thematic than Dominion and contains a lot better art that reminds me a bit of early MtG. I like Dominion better, but this one is definitely better than Thunderstone, which is even more thematic. Also, like Dominion again, this really benefits from adding the expansions. Best played as a 2-player game. The solo option is not super challenging, but requires a very different mindset from playing against a real person as you have to focus much more on controlling the center row than the card advantage.

RATING: 10
Ascension on the other hand streamlines the rules to a point where anyone with a passing knowledge of board games or CCG's can learn the game rapidly but they brought over the complexities and changing challenges from the Magic Pro Tour scene and I think it works wonderfully.  You need to strategize but constantly re-evaluate your decisions as the cards available change constantly.  You also have to consider what your opponents can and cannot handle on the board - if there's something they want it may be worth purchasing something you don't want if it would help them - or leaving something you may want so you don't bring up anything new.

RATING: 6.5
I can't help but compare this to Dominion.

First of all, the theme is better. Rather than building an empire that you can't see grow (only your deck gets bigger), you recruit heros and build artifacts that come to your aid.

Unlike Dominion, this game does not have "VP cards" that don't do anything else. Dominion's mechanic of "don't buy too many VPs or your ability cards will dilute" was clever, but it was always disappointing to draw VP cards.

Ascension has a go-to way to get points (Cultist). Additionally, the game's best feature is that you can combat monsters, which give you immediate rewards and are removed from play rather than going to your deck for some bonuses later.

Where Ascension falls short is its rulebook, which omits certain rules of play (when do Center Row cards get replaced? How can you divvy power? Can you really kill multiple Cultists?), and that the Cultist card is a bit too exploitable. Killing these multiple times in one turn is an efficient way to make many points.

Also there's something to be said about the card art, most of which seriously looks like it was drawn with colored pencils onto ruled notebook paper.

RATING: 3
After a video review

Very simple deck building game that does not match rune age for me. Same problem than legendary where the cards in the middle change all the time. Very annoying because you cannot plan strategy ahead and you constantly need to read new cards.

After playing the app

Very boring game with no objective. The game play could be summarized as: buy cards, to buy cards, to buy more cards to ... oh! wait I can kill a monster and get resources to buy even more cards. Entirely boring, it's deck building for deck building.

As a comparative, in "Star Realms", you build a deck to destroy your opponent, so you have a clear goal to achieve, here you just build your deck until all the points are distributed. Sure you can kill monsters, but most of the time, there are no monsters to kill and you end up killing dozens of cultist instead.

Now compared to Star Realm, the card interaction is much more complex and you can build powerful combo, I managed for example to play 11 cards when normally you play only 5. But it does not feel rewarding to build such combos because there is no good reason to do so. In fact, I ended up killing 8 cultists because there was nothing else to kill

I imagine people who like complex card combo would like this, else I don't see much to this game.

RATING: 8
While the great deckbuilding game hasn‚Äôt been created yet (sorry Dominion fans), Ascension:Chronicle of the Godslayer is a good step in the right direction. It plays quite quickly (if a bit slower than Dominion) and it trades off some luck in exchange for fun, meaningful decisions throughout the game and a purpose beyond fiddling with cards (namely, defeating monsters).

The result is a fun, yet flawed game that I‚Äôve played more than a dozen times face-to-face plus any number of games via the iOS app. It‚Äôs starting to wear a bit thin now (I haven‚Äôt gotten to check out the expansion yet), but I‚Äôm happy with it.

It avoids the two largest traps Dominion fell into: frontloading all of the interesting decisions and making the game about nothing more than fiddling with the cards. The former it cures with the randomly drawn tableau, and it comes at the price of a healthy dose of luck ‚Äì if the right card is drawn right before your turn you can be off to the races and having a hand tilted toward runes when it‚Äôs all monsters available (or vice versa) is a bummer. Also, there is a mild-to-moderate rich-get-richer problem since lucky breaks make it easier to take advantage of subsequent lucky breaks. It‚Äôs not so bad with more players, since there is a higher chance that another player has the luck and tools to keep up, but being left behind is no fun.

Since it‚Äôs hard to win the game without defeating some significant monsters along the way, your focus is not just on acquiring card which help you acquire more cards. This secondary note gives the game more variability than Dominion and is welcome. While Ascension still suffers from long turns caused by deck-churning combos, they do not seem to reach the epic proportions of Dominion‚Äôs worst excesses.

I think Ascension would have been better with separate monster and ‚Äúrunes‚Äù decks and tableaus, so both paths are always viable. Also, the economies of scale for paying higher prices are a little out of whack, since the designers and developers did not seem to take into account the value of getting an effect using a single card in hand versus relying on multiple cards in hand. Nevertheless, there are enough viable strategies, and the factions have enough distinct flavour, to keep the game worth exploring for many plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is definitely a game that does better in the lower player counts. With 4 the central row of cards to buy and fight change so much between one of your turns to the next that it is difficult to create a deck with cohesive synergies. I liked that almost every card gave you some VPs so that you didn't have to buy straight up VP cards. I would also like that there is always at the very least a cultist, mystic and heavy infantry to buy/beat up if it didn't seem like they were sometimes necessary for the game to work well. Finally the constructs I find pretty neat. 

However I am biased towards deck builders that have pre existing stacks for you to buy from rather than an ever changing one, and even in the dynamic card market I prefer Legendary since the heroes you can recruit changes each game there are also schemes.

EDIT after playing this over a hundred times against the  app AI: As always I find deckbuilders really addicting, but this one has so many flaws. I really think this game should havr considered having both money buy row and an attack buy row, as the difference between not having something to buy/attack in the center row and having a good option is pretty swingy. This is exacerbated by the fact there are very few center row scrappers in the game. 

Constructs are prototype-level in terms of execution and were much improved through Star Realms's bases. The inconsistency in when you have the ability to destroy your opponent's constructs can make them insanely powerful or near useless.

While there is non-static time-dependent value in terms of certain cards being worth more/less depending on how early/late the game is, that is also true for games with static setups, and here the lack of cards that combo with other cards make them have very static game-to-game value.

The mystics, heavy militia, and cultists are there more out of necessity to prevent the game from becoming degenerate than as an interesting option.

-	The art is *almost* worse than Dominion.

RATING: 5
This one actually has quite a bit in common with Dominion and Thunderstone with some major differences:

- Multiple buys allowed in a round
- Multiple combats allowed in a round
- Randomized row of six cards available for purchase
- 3 Standard cards always available
- Row refills immediately following combat and purchases

It's much less predictable and strategic than Dominion but plays with the same light feel and fast pace. It also remains VERY COMBOLICIOUS.

I'll be excited to see where they take this system next.

RATING: 4
[microbadge=13167][microbadge=670]

There is very little good to say about Ascension.  Obviously, it has a tough act to follow with Dominion.  However, other perfectly good games managed to make a distinctive splash (Rune Age, Core Worlds, Trains).  Ascension lacks any cohesive deck construction strategy; combos that arise are accidental at best, and there is never any question of how to play your hand.  The shifting center row seems like a good idea if you're bored of the static ten stacks Dominion offers, but it is implemented poorly; Ticket to Ride had a better implemented center row.  In the end, I can't imagine choosing this game over any other deck builder or card game.

RATING: 8
Judging solely from a couple of two-played games, Ascension appears to be a more successful variation of Dominion‚Äôs groundwork than Thunderstone, especially since it heeds to its ancestors lesson about the importance of clean design and good development. Although the ever-changing variety of cards available to purchase or kill suggests more chaos and less strategy, the correlation of the four factions with specific capabilities makes planning worthwhile. We‚Äôll see how this holds up for three or four players.

The biggest difference to other deckbuilding games is probably less the cycling card pool, but the ability to kill or buy several cards at once in any order you want. This opens up a couple of tactical options and is done deftly enough to be relatively hassle-free.

Themewise, having a more exotic background than the routine fantasy world was a good decision, as was handing the illustration work to a single artist ‚Äì even though only a few individual pieces of art really succeed for me, having a coherent vision for the whole game is a huge benefit.
