=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
Interesting deck builder.  The mechanics are solid and simple.  The game is more tactical than strategic, thus giving you significant choices to make every turn.

So now for the Dominion comparison.  I came to this game after almost 700 plays of Dominion (through Hinterlands expansion), which is my standard for in-game deck builders (e.g., I'm not comparing this to SWCCG).  In Dominion, you check out the 10 kingdom cards and for the most part can plan out your deck right then and there, although sometimes your decisions will depend on what others buy.  In Ascension, all your decisions depend on those center cards and what the other players are buying/defeating. [i]Of course[/i], that being the case, it tends to be more luck-driven than Dominion, and it can be frustrating to feel like you can never make it to 6 runes for the good cards while your opponent picks them all up--but such is the nature of luck!  Anyway, it's a fun twist on the deck building concept, and I'm glad it's not just a total Dominion clone.  

As in Dominion, I love the simple rule set.  

I think the theme will probably turn off more people than Dominion's low-key medieval theme, but I like it.  I also like the artwork style.  Don't get me wrong:  Some of the cards are freakishly ugly, but thematically they all mesh together very well.  Here again, I'm happy for the game to be distinctly visually separate from Dominion.

However, I don't think I'll get anywhere close to 700 plays of this one, since the center deck is the same each time.  Still, maybe I'll get to 100? (I suppose if I buy the expansions I'll get more mileage out of it.)  

Overall, it's an enjoyable game and I'm glad I bought it.  I'd certainly recommend it to people who enjoy in-game deck building, as I do.

NOTE:  Have played many IOS games now.  I don't log them on BGG.

RATING: 5.5
I've played this quite a lot now, and it's pretty much fine but, for a game that's supposedly created by expert Magic players, it's not as clever as it thinks it is. 

The main problem is that it's all too easy to have no option but to load your deck with Heavy Infantry and Mystics because your hand is so limited and the random draw to fill the six slots on the tableau can leave you stuck if you've not drawn a balance of runes and strength into your 5-card hand. This can be a function of clever play by your opponents, but all too often it's just down to the card draw - you have a hand full of runes whilst the tableau is full of monsters requiring strength to defeat. A balance of runes and strength means you are limited to small increments unless you have cards in your and that allow stronger powers; therefore you need to buy weaker cards with powers in order to combo up to cards with stronger powers.

Now, this may not seem that different to Dominion et al, but in Dominion you have choice: buy any of the cards available that you can afford and adapt your strategy to suit. In Ascension, the optimal choice is almost always to buy/defeat the strongest card in the tableau, unless you have a particular combination you think you can use (such as taking a lifebound hero because you have a deck full of lifebound heroes and they tend to complement each other); however, the combos can be difficult to achieve if you don't dump weak cards - Militia and Novice for example.

Constructs are a neat idea, giving turn bonuses which can mitigate the hand randomness, and can be either powerful or frustrating in practice. Powers such as "You may put any Mechana construct into play immediately" (without first cycling through the discard pile) are useful only if there is a Mechana construct in the tableau (admittedly, there is a Construct that allows you to treat any Construct as a Mechana construct). 

Some other powers are much more useful, and there are some great cards in here, but the method of obtaining them affects the balance some. I just think there would be a better mix if there were more options on your turn or, if the options were to be limited, then less randomness.

Saying that, I've managed to win 32 of 35 games so far, so skill must play its part; but the random factor means that, whilst there are some nice ideas in here, it's ultimately a little unsatisfying.

Oh, and the artwork is just terrible.

[Edit:] Okay, so I've now played this over a hundred times and my question is: 'Why?'. This really is nothing more than an okay deck-builder with a huge injection of luck in the cards drawn into the central tableau. There are some really nice ideas, but they're hamstrung by the defeat/acquire/draw mechanism which means you can have a strong hand and achieve nothing - something a weak opponent can exploit by not churning cards in the centre, which is one of the game's better 'gamey' mechanisms. It's still true that you don't have to think much. Take or defeat the most powerful card you can afford. If you have anything left, take what you can. With some experience now, I think there may be just too many opportunities to dump stuff from your hand; plus it's one of those games that seems to finish just as you get a decent engine going.

Compared (inevitably) to Dominion: the gameplay is too chaotic, most of the cards feel too samey, I don't feel a lot of symmetry between the cards, and I don't like that you cycle through your deck less frequently.

This isn't to say Ascension is a bad game, it's not. It's just that with Dominion I feel like I'm captaining the ship, and with Ascension I feel like I'm along for the ride. I do not have the box.

After getting reacquainted via the iPad app, then acquiring Storm of Souls, I've reacquired the base game. It's light and the setup and teardown is easy. It's fun and I can enjoy it without having to think too hard, which is sometimes a nice change-up.

RATING: 8
This is my pick of the deck building/cycling games that I played in 2011...and I've played around 10.

What sets it apart is that the central board always has 6 cards on offer which can be acquired or killed (monsters). As such the central board is always changing and the players need to adapt quickly and consider whagt cards they wish to add to their derck to develop a strategy to earn maximum Honour (VPs) or cycle quickly to those powerful cards.

Some consider the game too random and the decisions of which cards to get too obvious. I disagree about the obvious choice issue but do concede that the game can be a little random. Some strategies are hard to build from the base game alone but the 1st expansion fixes this issue.

The base game is best with 2-players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This deckbuilding game is very similar to Dominion and Thunderstone. Players draw a deck of 5 cards, and use them to purchase items (heroes, constructs) or combat monsters. The stuff for purchase/cpmbat are 6 revealed cards in the middle. When one card is dealt with, another card is immediately revealed from the deck, so you can keep cycling through. Of course this cycling through leads to a bit more luck on what shows up. 

It's a fast paced game. I enjoyed my playing of it, and it is not as complex as Dominion. There is enough meat for strategy on buiding your deck, but there still is the luck factor to deal with.

RATING: 8
Ascension is deck-building game that appears inspired by Dominion. Players start with an initial hand of cards which allows purchase of new cards. Some cards are more powerful "currency" for future purchases, other cards allow the player special actions. The goal is to have collected the greatest score in trophies at the end of the game. The main differences are:
(1) there are six face-up cards in the middle of the table, available for purchase. These are replaced from a common stock as soon as they are sold. So the set of cards is constantly changing, and there is less fiddly setup than at the start of Dominion.
(2) there are two types of "money"; coins (runes?) used to buy cards, and power points that are used to defeat monsters.
(3) no limit on actions or purchases each turn.
(4) points are scored for defeating monsters, and some cards are worth additional points at the end of the game. Players compete for a common pool of points ("Honour"), the game ends when the common pool is depleted.
I like Ascension: it is faster to set-up than Dominion, and easier to teach. It also comes in a smaller box. On the other hand, Dominion is a deeper game with much greater variability. 
(Ascension's expansions are really "options" - you probably do not want to use more than one at a time. Each adds its own flavour with little increase in complexity, but they add to setup/cleanup time).

RATING: 8
A quick and clean deck building game. Like Thunderstone, you each start with a set deck and then have options to buy stuff or to kill stuff. The difference is that there are no decks to buy from. Instead there are only 6 cards displayed in the draft, a random mix of cards to buy and cards to kill, drawn from the the one central deck, so as your turn comes around, you get what you get and you don't get upset. Which makes for a more random deck build, but I actually enjoy (and may end up preferring) the unpredictability. Some turns you might get nothing, others there's some decent choices to be made. The stuff to kill are monsters and you get instant rewards (and opponents may be forced to discard stuff) and the stuff you buy could be heroes (which give any variety of purchase / kill power when you draw them back into your hand to play) and constructs (which once you draw and play stays out and generates good stuff each turn). Generally monsters and heroes get you VPs, constructs less so, so there's a choice there on which way to go. The game is easy to teach and it's quick to get up and going (an advantage over the others), but it's more random, sure. The overall variety from game to game may be lower (without the huge variety of card types that Dominion and Thunderstone provide) but it's perhaps made up for by having variety of deck thrust upon you intra-game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
"Very smooth 2-currency deckbuilder. Competitive, but has very few pvp attacks, and not much screwage. It is a high 7 for me, and is one of my favorite non-cooperative deckbuilders."

What I like:
- Two-currency deck-management game with unlimited buys
- Super simple and quick setup and teardown
- Plays quickly (45 minutes for 3p learning game)
- Very few pvp attacks

What I didn't like:
- Center row randomness is just a bit more than I like
- The card combos seem a bit less interesting than (for example) Legendary
- Graphic design is less functional than (for example) Legendary

This is a solid deck management game. Nothing amazing, but clean, simple, and quick. I would put the weight right around 2.0.

RATING: 10
My rating is influenced by my customizations. The base game by itself, played by the book, would be an 8.
House rules - Customized player powers (think DC Deckbuilding). Separate monster deck (no cultist). Center row consists of 5 cards from the heroes/constructs deck, and 3 cards from the monster deck.

Pros:
Lots of room for customization, especially with expansion components.
Quick to setup and start playing, unlike Dominion or Legendary.
Each faction offers a different strategy for winning the game (as do my custom player powers).

Cons:
Can be a long wait between turns, especially with more than 4p.
Hard to plan your turn in advance, as the center row constantly changes.
My custom player powers could use more playtesting (Pro: an excuse to play more boardgames).

edit: I have this on my phone now with all the expansions. No two games of this are alike but it's still pretty shallow it seems to me. I enjoy it enough to play on my device from time to time, but again I don't think it has the longevity as other similar titles have for me. I'll probably pick up any new expansion that comes out for it, but I doubt this game will ever make it to 100 plays.

I feel Star Realms is similar, but superior design to this one. ( how ever star realms can get dull if played to much as well)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Well, it's a deckbuilding game. An ok, maybe even fun diversion. Adds a twist to the genre in the cards you can gain come out randomly. I don't think that's a good twist. A large part of deckbuilding games is their varied setup, and coming up with a strategy that fits it. That's what makes cards vary in value; in one setup you might want card A, while in another setup B is superior. When cards come out randomly, there's no planning. And if card A is better than B, it will be so 90% of the time, the other 10% being the rare occasion when you've already acquired a card that goes really well with B.

There's already randomness and luck in deckbuilding games, in that you don't know in what order and combination your cards come out. You might really want that $8 card, but the cards in your hand only add up to $7. Bummer! When the cards for purchase come out randomly too, that's too much. Now you have that $7 and there isn't even a card that can be bought with $.

The experience is ok. Drawing cards, buying stuff, thrashing &c. is fine. Just know that you're at the mercy of the cards, and you have less influence over how you play than the person who shuffled the stack.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Most of my plays were iPadded, but there are some face-to-face plays in there as well. A completely tactical deckbuilder, in that there is zero point to planning ahead: you can't, not if you choose from among 6 cards each turn and build your deck piecemeal (as opposed to the Dominion-style complete market). Your turns are automatic: you perform whatever actions you can in the two forms of currency you can spend per turn (runes and strength), you buy the most expensive card you can, and things move along because the presence of the Cultist always gives you something to do; there are no "dead hands." 

Oddly addictive on the iPad but ultimately mindless. It also commits what I consider a major sin of modern gaming, which is to reward players for achieving strategic goals despite only giving them tactical tools to do so. In this case, being able to synergize your factions can create large action chains, but you can't plan to do that.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]RO[/b]: [i]02/26/2016[/i]

Ascension is my favorite deckbuilding game. For my money, it beats Dominion and Thunderstone any day of the week. Yes, I know many other deckbuilders have come out since and I must confess I have played none of them but there is something about Ascension that pleases me on a fundamental level. In Dominion and [i]especially[/i] Thunderstone deck bloat is a serious and aggravating problem, what I mean by this is you will often find yourself with cards in your hand that are completely, and utterly useless or even that hurt you (And if deck thinning is your thing, the Void class of cards in Ascension will get your deck nice and svelte in no time).

You could utter the admonition "git gud newb" but lets be honest here... I can probably kick your ass, so you shouldn't do that. Kidding aside, the fact that basically every card is useful every time you draw it in Ascension makes me very happy. Even IF it simply allows you to draw another card or something that isn't extremely useful in and of itself, it does [i]something[/i] or helps you [i]somehow[/i].

Ascension isn't without it's flaws of course. It has been said that the game is very luck-y and can "play itself". But I don't buy that entirely, knowledge of the deck and crunching probabilities is a big part of the game (this of course only possible after multiple plays). Did a lot of monsters come out early? Well, there probably won't be many more coming out so better shift your acquisitions to purchasing power over military. Got a couple mechana constructs? Hmm, should you pick up those cards that let you banish and cycle to deck to get the crushing mechana combo going? Or switch gears? There [i]is[/i] strategy in this game, and long term planning even if it is mostly a tactical game.

RATING: 7
I enjoy this game quite a bit. I think it's a great beginner deckbuilder because it isn't very complicated, but there is a decent amount of depth with this game. The expansions that I've played add a lot of complexity to this game. It's a good game that seems to be overshadowed by other deckbuilders, but it's still worth playing.

RATING: 7
While playing Ascension I'm reminded of a lot of other cards games. It's easy to see that this comes from the minds of Magic the Gathering players, but what this really resembles is Thunderstone. One thing Ascension holds over these other card games is that it plays much smoother. Even with expansions and up to six players, the game doesn't really get bogged down,except maybe towards the very end when people start to have really complicated decks. Keep in mind, the more players you have the more luck gets involved, seeing as the available cards will almost completely refresh before your turn comes around again. Either way, this is probably my current favorite deck-builder.

RATING: 8
Rating for the iOS digital adaptation, which is superb (lots and LOTS of live and async online plays)

Joint rating for the base set [Chronicle of the Godslayer] and Return of the Fallen [RotF], as this is my preferred way to play Ascension.

Ascension is a dark fantasy deck-builder, that has been ridiculed for its laughably shoddy art-work style. Once you get past the art-work, Ascension is a fast-paced, excellent deck-builder that has different routes to victory (moreso when playing with RotF with the base set), with a number of different considerations: which card(s) to purchase, board-state, on-board and off-board honour points, what strategy is your opponent following, the rate at which honour points are decreasing, and card assessment in the beginning, mid, and late game.

Detractors claim that Ascension is a luck-fest where winning is a simple matter of purchasing the highest cost card from the 6 center cards available. This is completely and wholly false, and ignores all of the above considerations, greatly underplaying the importance of understanding board-state and card assessment. 

The digital adaptation by Playdek, and online play is so good - and introduced me to async gaming - that I will never play the physical version.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This is a "deck building" game less about building a deck than cobbling one together based on limited, random options each turn.

RATING: 6
[color=#CC0000]

I expected god-slaying.  There was no god-slaying in my copy.

This game would absolutely jump with a first-to-the-top endgame condition.  Get as tough as you can as quickly as you can, then claim the throne.  The player on the throne gets a defense bonus for anyone attacking him.  Each player goes for the throne when they choose; last man on the throne wins.  That should-I-stay-or-should-I-go aspect would add some heat to what is otherwise an accounting session.  

Honestly, this is half a game.  Given its theme and overall concept it needs something to pull the players together.

S.

RATING: 8
In my opinion, the best of the deckbuilding games. Supplanted Dominion for me, and Thunderstone didn't have a chance, although I like both.

This game beats both of those due to its quick set up time and its more poker-like skill requirements. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My introduction to the deckbuilding genre.  I like the quick and easy setup of Ascension, with the center row of cards.  

The monsters in the center row make for an interesting balance of purchasing and combat, though everything is worth points in the end.  I find the concept of making your deck as efficient as possible intriguing, though I'm still learning the nuances of it.  

Ascension has a few problems, though, which makes me think it will fade in favor of other deckbuilders (like Star Realms).  I like the idea of the factions but I don't feel they had enough of an identity, mechanically or thematically, to really stand out.  I also think the factions are imbalanced, as the player who gathers the most Mechana's is most likely to win because of their huge point values (and gaining Mechana's makes it easier to gather other Mechana's).  

Finally, I'll beat the dead horse:  this game's artwork is atrocious, some of the worst I've ever seen in a mainstream release.  I really feel this hurts the game, as I'm not drawn in by the art, it doesn't add much theme, and it's so ugly I feel no desire to acquire cards beyond their mechanical value.  Really missed opportunity there.  

Disclaimer:  I've only played the base game, no expansions.  I have heard the artwork and factions are both improved in some of them, but I can't attest to it personally.

RATING: 6.5
Plays track games against human opponents and do not count playing against the app AI.

Ascension is a fun deckbuilding game for 2 players with more strategy than many give it credit for. Additional players leads to too little control and planning due to turnover of the center row, and too much downtime. The game is more tactical than strategic, but there is an enormous amount of replayability. I've played the app against the AI thousands of times, which is pretty much the best way to play the game. Ascension also works as a 4 player partner game using a variant for 2 center rows, though it's a bit slow that way.

In the first block, mechana constructs are too strong, and many of the low cost cards aren't really worth taking.

In the second block, there is a focus on trophy monsters, and they added soul gems, which are an extremely random bonus. Mechana constructs are a little too weak, lifebound is a little too strong, and the card abilities are a little more diverse. Soul gems add an extra element of randomness that I'm not the biggest fan of, but it's functional. The low cost cards tend to be better, than in the first block.

In the third block, energy is a 3rd resource which is needed to trigger certain abilities. The way treasure works gives you an incentive to sometimes take cards you normally wouldn't, but if you never have energy when you need it, it can be frustrating. Many of the cards permanently transform if you have enough energy, making the low cost cards much more interesting. The factions seem relatively balanced.

In the fourth block, there are multi faction cards, a much bigger emphasis on playing faction cards, and Mystics and Heavy Infantry are even worse than in the other blocks as a a result. Low cost cards are much more interesting and valuable, and there is greater potential for chaining things together. Enlightened is the strongest faction, but the balance between the factions is better than in the first 2 blocks. You start with an affiliation with one of the factions, and the card you get as a bonus for the enlightened one is much weaker than the cards for the other affiliations, but getting to rally enlightened is better than rally any of the other factions, so I suspect those balance out.

Overall the later blocks are more random than the earlier blocks, and I suspect that the first 2 blocks are the best if you want a strategy game, though you're better off with Puzzle Strike or something if you want strategy.

RATING: 7.5
Fast-playing deck builder with a MtG meets Dominion (there's mana and combat) gameplay.

Players use their rune cards (mana) to buy cards to enhance their abilities to building power to beat up monsters(for VPs). Stuff purchased (economic-engine route) also grants VPs.

Rinse and repeat and you have Ascension.

Rating based on iOS version. All such deck builders should be played via iPads connected wirelessly...it really reduces the downtime with shuffling etc.

RATING: 3
I wanted to like it, but I found few decisions to be had. The big problem is that there is no limit to the number of buys that you have.  That means that you just buy what is available to you, and the player that has the best cards available to them when they have the right hand will be better for it.  Most of the buys were obvious, so you just bought the best things that you could get.  Ultimately, this means that the player that had the opportunity to buy the best cards early on will be rewarded by being able to have more flexibility to buy more cards later in the game.  There isn't much that you can do to further your game when the cards that come out don't match your hand currency.  So the majority of the game revolves around how lucky you are to have good cards available to you that you can buy.  The whole thing feels like it plays you more than you playing it. Disappointing.

RATING: 6.5
I don't find this game very interesting, but my wife loves it. It wouldn't be in my collection anymore if it weren't for that fact. 

The game is too tactical for my tastes. There is not much you can look into strategically, other than the small display of cards. There are two major strategic arcs to explore (constructs vs. monster killing) with maybe a couple of sub-arcs (which faction to concentrate on, how much to invest into each major arc, etc) depending upon what shows up. That is my main beef: the game is entirely dependent upon what shows up. Other than the two main arcs, I don't see much room for strategic exploration. Sure, you may figure out which card combos work best, but that's about it. 

Maybe some expansions would correct this, but I don't want to put more money into a gaming system I don't generally love in the first place. There are many other deck builders that I get more enjoyment out of (Dominion, Trains, Valley of the Kings, Study in Emerald). This is not my go-to game for the genre.

RATING: 7.4
Components - 1/1 Deck building game, the cards are cards, and the board/gems provided are fine. The wife absolutely loves the gems (reminds her of bejeweled which she loves)

Fun - .7/1 A little harsh, as this is my wife's favorite and thus we've played back to back to back to... I'm a little burned on it but overall I've loved this better than Dominion (the only other deck builder I've played at the time of this writing). It ultimately has more interaction than Dominion as well.

Replayability - .7/1 Deck is big enough to allow for many replays

Strategic - .7/1 A lot of luck, what cards are turned up on your turn, what you get in your deck, but these are common for the genre. Smart deck building will mitigate some of the luck, in addition there is plays that you will do to block other players, or combos to give you loads of victory points. It's a balance between short term and long term goals.

Theme - .3/1 Honestly, I just don't feel much of it when playing. I feel like the power/runes/monsters/heroes are sort of plug and play. I think it's more me (particular for deckbuilding, Domainion is even worse to me in this regard) than anything else.


Personal Review - 4/5 Prior to the wife and I playing this to death, I really did enjoy this game a lot, now it's still fun, just a little worn. There is a ton of expansions and they seem to help keep even more options in the game, though I'm trying to keep this review to just the core game.

RATING: 6
It's a deck-building game like Dominion, but with several differences:
- no fixed tableau of (10 out of ... fiftinfinity) cards to purchase; instead, all cards are shuffled into a deck and become available randomly (about 8? available at any time)
- 3 things (2 cards, and a way to purchase VP tokens) are _always_ available
- 2 different currencies (silver and red)
- some cards stay out permanently and have ongoing effects

But also a few similarities:
- VP tokens (as added in ... Seaside?)
- Equally unthematic. Godslayer? Really? No, these were just cards in different colors and different costs.

Overall, I prefer Dominion, esp. Prosperity. You're building a deck here, but you can't really build a strategy: cards come up randomly so you have to play entirely tactically. Really, it felt like I simply bought whatever I could afford at the moment. I'm sure (I hope, anyway) that with repeated play and better knowledge of the cards in the game, there would be some basis for choosing which particular cards to buy (on those rare occasions when you can afford more than one of the cards on display). But I don't expect to seek this out again, although I would be willing to try it again if someone else wanted to try the game.

RATING: 7
Quick deckbuilding game playable solo, with fast setup and takedown. Often compared to Thunderstone, yet I find the two very different, Ascension being a lot more random. The cards you buy in Thunderstone are a fixed pool determined at the beginning of the game, whereas in Ascension, there is only one pool of cards that includes both monsters and allies. It is a different mechanic that makes for quicker yet more random games, but I enjoy both. When there is little time for a game, Ascension is the better choice and still very entertaining with some nice combos.

RATING: 6.5
This is a cool take on the new "deck building" craze.  "Dominion" was fun while it lasted, but it lost its lustre as time went on, and the effort to set up the game prevented it from being played as often as it could have been.  Fortunately, with "Ascension", setup is a breeze and players can dive right into the game.  I also enjoy the fact that it is a slightly more tactical game than "Dominion" (even if it is less strategic) since there is a smaller and ever-changing assortment of cards to choose from.  There are also some fun new elements, such as the Rune/Power points, the Construct cards, and the victory points on the cards.  One downfall of the game is that there seems to be even less player interaction than in "Dominion".  And the artwork is even more hideously ugly than that of "Dominion", if you can imagine that.  Not to mention the very strange "theme", which involves the most bizarre mix of mechanical and fantasy beings...  But to end on a positive note, I do enjoy those acrylic gems!  Sparkly!  Overall, a fast and fun game.  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 6.5/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dismissed this one early on as yet another Dominion clone with dodgy art, but the art has grown on me -- and, returning to it, I find it fixes everything I hate most about other deckbuilders.

By way of context, I like Dominion a lot as a design. I have logged several hundred plays of the Android implementation and [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/blogpost/538/review-battle-of-gundabad]iOS clone[/url] -- but I find it unbearable as a physical card game. Partly because it's dry as the Sahara, mostly because it takes as long to set up and put away as it does to play, which only makes sense if you play more than one game in a row. In that case, I'd rather play one game of something longer.

Ascension fixes the "setup problem" by replacing the multiple draw piles with a central tableau. There are three always-available cards but otherwise players share an ever-changing array of 6, drawn from a single stack. This increases the variety of cards available through the game, though it obviously also increases the randomness -- you may well feel victim to the whims of the deck.

But to reiterate, setup time is about 30 seconds.

Many of the usual deckbuilding issues apply, e.g. that downtime increases with the number of players and it largely feels like a "multiplayer solitaire" experience. Unlike other deckbuilders, the draw randomness can make it frustratingly difficult to exploit card synergies; you can't build an engine if the game is determined not to let you.

That all suits me because the niche it fills in my collection is that of two-player "super filler". (It does take more but two is the sweet spot for both strategy and speed.) It's strategic enough to maintain interest, and it's quick to set up, quick to explain and quick to put away.

I traded it because I don't have much call for two player fillers and 99% of my matches were on iOS.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Initial rating 8. Could go up. 

I am a disciple of the Church of Dominion, but this is really, really, really good. Granted, it is much lighter than the big 'D' and there is not much room for strategic play. But I am all about tactical and even more about fun, and this presses both of those buttons. If you think of Dominion as 'steak and baked potatoes' then maybe Ascension is 'burger and fries.' Both yummy. But one is more substantial and the other is great for quick, easy, on-the-go sustenance that might make you a little gassy, but what the hell.

Recommended, unless you really want/need to be able to formulate a strategy. I just don't think that's here. Read some of the 3-4 rating reviews and maybe play before you buy. I can't argue with the low ratings because alot of what they say is spot on. But they're looking for different things in the game than I am, and that's okay.

The artwork, however, is strictly amateur hour. Remember having that friend in high school who could kinda draw and his colored-pencil monsters would be sorta cool but you knew deep down that it wasn't that great? This is that.

RATING: 9
This is the game I wanted Thunderstone to be.  It's essentially Dominion with two forms of currency: runes to buy things that improve your deck and fighting strength to kill monsters for both points and immediate one-shot beneficial effects.  The rules are actually simpler than Dominion and the card effects quite well balanced (in contrast to Thunderstone).  And despite lacking an analog to attack cards, the game is far more interactive.  One of the better games I've played in a while.

RATING: 3.5
First Impression:

A simple deck building game that emphasises in strange theme and art.

So what's good about this game; Well the art might appeal to some (I detest it), and it plays fairly quickly. There some interesting combos but in order to pull them off you'll need a lot of luck.

Make that tons of luck actually. Offering different options in each players turn is not working here. They managed to take a skill-based mechanic and completely ruin it with merciseless luck of the draw. The end result is dissapointing. Players will do better or worse clearly depending on the whim of the deck, and thus the whole strategy part of the deck building falls apart and leaves only the shuffling and counting. If deck building was implemented like this when it first appeared, it wouldn't have even earned it's place among the other mechanics. I could continue describing other elements of this game that I don't like, like the art or the theme but these are all minor for a game that just doesn't play well.

I really dislike this game, pretty much all aspects of it.

Update: Having played Star Realms, I now know this particular variant of deck building Ascension created works. Just not for Ascension though!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Adds some twists to the Dominion deck building model, but ultimately this is less my style than its ancestor. While Dominion is a puzzle where you decide on a strategy when you see the pool of available cards and then play the game out to see the effects of that strategy, Ascension is an almost entirely tactical exercise of buying the best available card(s) from those that are randomly available on your turn. After a few plays these tactical decisions become automatic once you have grasped the relative strengths of the various cards. The game description promises something new, exciting and better than Dominion, but the description ends up as the most interesting part of the game, which is the very definition of ho hum.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[thing=69789][/thing] is a deck builder, but not my favorite kind of deck builder.

In your turn, you will use runes (money) to buy new cards, and attack to defeat monsters and gain honor (victory points). Everyone starts with 8 Apprentice (1 rune) and 2 Militia (1 attack). There are two basic cards that are always up for purchase: Heavy Infantry (2 attack) and Mystic (2 runes). Also, there will be always a Cultist card (needs attack 2 to be defeated) - the player can always use attack to beat the Cultist, gaining 1 honor each time.

All the other cards will come from the main deck. The cards up for purchase or to be fought will be in a central row. When a card is purchase or beaten, a new one will take its place. As usual the cards will have many effects, and I guess that all of the monsters, when defeated, will provide some sort of one time effect to be used in the same turn the player defeated the monster - things like: draw cards, banish a card from the row, gain runes, and, of course, all of them will give honor.

The game will proceed until all the honor (25 per player) ends (but players will still gain honor, there are more tokens, basically the same thing that happens in [thing=28143][/thing]). Then it will be the final round (everyone will have the same number of turns). The player with the highest amount of honor (from tokens and also from the cards - most of the cards are also worth honor) will be the winner.

The row for the cards to come in once one is fought or bought can create that sort of situation that I don't like: you got 7 to buy, but only cards of 3 or 4 value are there. So you take one of these, than a card, that you really would want, shows up, costing 6. Well, now you are screwed - specially because this great card will probably be taken by someone else.

I don't mind all that much, but this is far from optimal. Yes, the set up is made shorter this way (just shuffle everything together and you are good to go), and also the breakdown (just separate the basic cards out the decks). But this also makes strategies harder to be effective. I once bought two good cards to use on Mechanas, but none other where there when my turn was up.

Nonetheless, [thing=69789][/thing] is an easy game to get going, to teach and to play. For the art, I liked it quite a bit - it is thematic and sets a fine mood - I see some complains regarding it, but, for me, is really well done.

*****

After playing a lot - a lot - of games in the app, I can see much better the possibilities and fine tunning of the game. I enjoy it much more now, and even increased its rating from 6 to 7.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Good:
-Clean design with easy rules
-Gateway game
-Cool card interactions
-Always something to do on your turn
-No significant player interaction
-More casual than most deck builders
-Unpredictability of the center row

Bad:
-Unpredictability of the center row
-Greatly improved by expansion
-Terminology can be confusing to new gamers
-Down time

Overall:
It is unfair to compare it to Dominion.  Dominion is more strategic.  The winner is the player who identified the best was to pair the cards, manage their deck, and draw well.  While Ascension is more tactical.  You buy the card that best fits into your deck from the limited choices that are constantly changing, maximize the points you can earn, and put yourself in a good position for future turns.  While Dominion is a tighter and better design with significantly less luck, it lacks the charm of Ascension.  It comes down to, do you want a game where the best strategy wins (Dominion) or one where the winner is the person who is playing well and lucky.

RATING: 7
A strong example of the deckbuilding genre of games.

Quicker to play than Dominion and Thunderstone.  It distinguishes itself in two ways.  

One, it moves the choices from the "strategic" to the "tactical."  With the other games in this genre, you often make most of your important decisions at the very start of the game when you choose your course of action for assembling your deck.  With Ascension, doing that is a route to failure and would probably make the game seem overly simplistic and boring.

Two, the board itself is more random, but the decks themselves are less random.  With other deckbuilding games, the composition of available cards doesn't change or only partially changes throughout the game.  With Ascension, the board changes virtually every turn, but in a random fashion.  On the flipside, Ascension's game mechanics focus much more heavily on filtering lower-quality cards out of your deck as compared to other games in the genre.

This game stands out for me because of the quickness, the ease of setting up and taking down, and the tactical depth and number of choices per minute offered to the player.  It's a different beast than Dominion - if you want the strategic "decide your buys before you start playing" nature of Dominion or Thunderstone, you probably won't like it.

RATING: 7
It's funny that this entire sub-genre of deckbuilding games with a replenishing, changing area of cards to buy (rather than Dominion's supply piles) seemed to have spawned from Ascension, when the mechanic itself was something that the designer of Dominion considered but scrapped!

It adds quite a bit of luck to the genre, but that might not be such a bad thing. Although with many players this aspect does seem to become a bit overwhelming, and it becomes impossible to plan as the communal card area changes completely by the time your turn comes around again.

In any case, Ascension is fun.

RATING: 8
Oct 2017: Rating -1 (to 8)
Lowering my rating of the base game only because I don't play this as often as the War of Shadows edition.  Valley of the Ancients is also a great game if you're playing 3-4P.  I think Mystic Vale still reigns as my favorite deckbuilder, but I would have to place Ascension above Dominion now.  The theme is fun, the monsters add a different element (even if power really is just a different currency), and your score at the end depends upon both your honor points (gems) and the honor on all the cards you've collected.  That means virtually every card you pick up matters - not just in how your game plays out but in your final score.  Sometimes it's worth picking up a high point card you don't *need* just to get those points.  The different factions and synergy between them also takes it a stop above Dominion, because they contribute to your overall strategy.  (Especially if you're using an expansion that has things like Unite powers.)

Original, Dec 2016:
One of my favorite games and probably falls right behind Dominion for my favorite deck builder.

Art is great, theme plays across very well.  I love that it incorporates two elements: buying (runes) and kill monsters (power).  Sometimes the cards that lay out on the board will have no monsters when you have a bunch of power, and sometimes there won't be anything useful you can buy.  There's some luck there, but otherwise it's largely strategy.  Do you want to focus on buying valuable cards that yield honor points every turn or at the end of the game?  Or maybe you'd rather focus heavily on military power and kill monsters for your honor points?  It seems like a balance between the two usually pans out best, but we've had games where one person does very well just buying tons of constructs and other games where one person does well building up a strong military.

My parents also love this game.  It was slightly confusing at first and they still have to ask "what does this card do?" sometimes, but it's one they've added to their own collection and enjoy playing alone.  I'd say this plays well with 2-4 players.  2 players is faster but still fun.  4 players can drag out a little if you have indecisive people, but gets better as everyone playing knows the game.

Would like to get Return of the Fallen and possibly War of Shadows.

RATING: 7
The common simplification floating around about this is "[gameid=36218] with a dark fantasy theme," which is pretty accurate  There's an additional type of currency, and there aren't the restrictions to one buy, but the core mechanic of buy cards to put into your deck so you can buy better cards is unchanged.  I think this one is probably a bit more forgiving and luck-driven, and it's not so easy to find an easy strategy from the beginning of the game, which I actually prefer.  You're constantly assailed with new choices as new cards get flipped over, which means the game doesn't feel like a long resolution after the card set is revealed at the beginning like Dominion does.

The theme for this game also helps.  This feels like a real world, with the factions each having a cohesive feel.  Even the sometimes campy colored-pencil art that looks like it came straight out of a high-school notebook helps.  I'll always prefer a real setting, as opposed to the generic crap that Dominion does.  Duchy? Pfft.

RATING: 7
There are things I like about this game, and there are things that I don't like about this game.  The weird thing is that those things are both related to the main core mechanism of the game, which is the single row of cards that's available to players, instead of having a full tableau of different cards from which to choose.  On the one hand, I think it's a nice alternative to Dominion, where you wind up getting a few cards that aren't as useful as you want them to be; on the other hand, it's a little annoying to draw a hand full of attack cards, and have nothing but triangles in the row.  The use of the Mystics, Fighters, and Cultists allow for something to always be available, and there's a nice balance of getting points from killing monsters and getting points for buying cards, but sometimes you want something better.  It becomes more of a tactical game than a strategic one, and ultimately it comes down to developing a good strategy based on the luck of the draw.  As such, I think it probably plays better as a 2-player game than a multiplayer one.  Still, it's a fun game that owes a lot to Dominion without feeling like that's the case.  Or maybe I'm just accustomed to shuffling a whole bunch of cards in games by now.

Hoopty-jillion plays: 2 players (iOS)
3 plays: 3 players
2 plays: 4 players

RATING: N/A
Ascension is a fast paced deckbuilding game designed by Magic‚Ñ¢ Pro Tour champions Justin Gary, Rob Dougherty, and Brian Kibler, with artwork by Eric Sabee. Ascension is a deck-building game where players spend Runes to acquire more powerful cards for their deck. It offers a dynamic play experience where players have to react and adjust their strategy accordingly.
\13\\13\
Gameplay is similar to other deckbuilding games, with the player adding cards to their deck by purchasing them from a central deck, which has the top six cards revealed and available for purchase. When cards are removed, they are replaced with a new one from the top of the deck. Victory points are earned in three ways: firstly, each card purchased is worth one or more victory points; second, victory points can be gained by defeating monsters with a power resource featured on several of the cards; finally, some cards give victory points directly each time they are played.

RATING: 7
I've played over 800 games of Ascension on iOS, with various combinations of expansions, and all with friends I know in meatspace. The Playdek app is really fantastic--the best card game app I've played.

Ascension itself is an OK-to-pretty-good deckbuilder. It seems silly to be so lukewarm on a game I've played so much, but I really think it's the convenience of being able to make a move in any random moment (in 5-6 concurrent games) that got me here, rather than a particular strength of the game itself.

The changing board makes the game more dynamic than Dominion in the sense that you can't solve it ahead of time, then just execute your plan. On the other hand, the changing board rarely leads to hard decisions, so it adds more randomness than tactical depth.

All that said, I'm still enjoying the game after all these plays, when many other game apps have come and gone. It's light but pleasant, and so so easy to pick up, take a few turns, and put away.

RATING: 6
As a pure deck-building game, it isn't bad. It distinguishes itself from Dominion by having two forms of currency. But when boiled down to the basics, it's still a very average and uninspired experience to play.

The iOS version is actually much preferable because it strips away all of the annoying elements of deck-builders (setup, shuffling, score-tallying) and leaves you with a very quick, distilled game of building card combos.

Again, I don't think Ascension is deficient in any particular respect except that it has been overshadowed for me by other games that offer more theme and more interesting game play (Legendary). As such, I consider it very "middle of the road".

[u]Update:[/u] I finally did it. I broke the game engine. I played a game where I cycled the entire deck and reached 2305 points before I had to shut it off due to the late hour. I probably could have gone considerably higher than that. I had easily 170 cards in my deck, 46 constructs on the table and had accumulated over 2700 combat thanks to a pair of Moon Staves. Yep. Broke the game. It was pretty cool to watch and I'm sad I didn't get to play the rest of it out.

RATING: 4
After 5+ Plays: I'm not the world's biggest dominion fan, but I can see the cleverness in the design.  This game removes that cleverness. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Ascension is a bastard child of Thunderstone. The mechanics are largely similar, but the game itself is much easier to teach and learn. You get some mana and some attack, then use it to buy/kill things in the centre row, which is 6 cards that are drawn from a single draw deck.

Unlike Thunderstone, the deck buffs and the monsters come from the same deck. This gives you less control over how you shape your deck, as at times there may only be 1 or 2 non-supply deck buff cards that you can buy. The single draw deck can sometimes stall a game even further by having an early stream of expensive cards or hard-to-kill monsters, leaving all the players unable to properly streamline their deck with improvements in order to proceed.

One advantage to Ascension is how easy it is to teach/learn. If you are teaching someone that has played nearly any deckbuilder before, the instructions last about 30 seconds.

I like Ascension best with 2 players, as I find with more than that your centre row control goes right out the window. With 2 players it is more like a tactical duel, as any time you buy/kill something, you're freeing up a centre row spot that might give your opponent something awesome. With more than 2 the centre row is much less stable, and sometimes with 4 players you won't even see many of the cards that cycle through.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
